# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** kubernetes/kubernetes (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.


## Artifact-Only Evaluation

You are in **artifact-only mode**. Your workspace is empty — all code discovery
must go through Sourcegraph MCP tools. Express all code changes as **unified
diffs** in your output artifact (e.g., `fix_patch` fields in review.json, or a
standalone `solution.patch` file). Do NOT attempt to edit source files directly
— there are no source files in your workspace.

The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. ## Summary

The document provides an advanced architecture and operations guide covering:

### **Core Sections**

1. **Subsystem Boundaries** - Upstream dependencies (apimachinery, api), downstream consumers (operators, kubectl, controllers), and responsibility boundaries

2. **Key Components** (with file evidence):
   - **REST Client** (rest/config.go, client.go, transport.go): Configuration management, request building, transport layer with auth
   - **Typed & Dynamic Clients** (kubernetes/clientset.go, dynamic/, discovery/): Compile-time safe access vs. runtime flexibility
   - **Controller Infrastructure** (tools/cache/): Reflector for LIST/WATCH, DeltaFIFO for event queueing, SharedInformer for caching and notification
   - **Work Queue** (util/workqueue/): Decouples event detection from reconciliation with exponential backoff
   - **Informer Factory** (informers/): Multiplexes multiple resource type watchers

3. **End-to-End Control Flow** - Traced a Pod modification scenario from watch stream through DeltaFIFO, event handler, work queue, to controller reconciliation with concrete code paths

4. **Failure Modes** - Watch disconnection recovery, "too old resourceVersion" handling, handler panics, queue overload, and race conditions with mitigation strategies

5. **Extension Points** - Custom ListerWatchers, indexing functions, TransformFuncs, rate limiters, event handlers, transport middleware, and server-side apply

6. **Operational Implications**:
   - Performance: Watch stream efficiency vs. polling costs
   - Reliability: Watch disconnection handling and resync
   - Memory: Caching trade-offs and scaling strategies
   - Correctness: Eventual consistency mod

{
  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes@c18ca078466118135fcab744ab3fe6e47c94a97d/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",

    "content": "1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ",

PRs that are\\n50: cherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\\n51: to the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\\n52: changing the patch version.\\n53: \\n54: #### Branches and tags.\\n55: \\n56: We will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\\n57: number. (E.g., the clock for the release-2.0 branch started ticking when we\\n134: tagged v2.0.0, not when we made the first alpha.) PRs that are\n50: cherry-picked into an older Kubernetes release branch will result in an update\n51: to the corresponding branch in `client-go`, with a corresponding new tag\n52: changing the patch version.\n53: \n54: #### Branches and tags.\n55: \n56: We will create a new branch and tag for each increment in the minor version\n57: number. We will create only a new tag for each increment in the patch\n58: version number. See [semver](http://semver.org/) for definitions of major,\n59: minor, and patch.\n60: \n61: The HEAD of the master branch in client-go will track the HEAD of the master\n62: branch in the main Kubernetes repo.\n63: \n64: #### Compatibility: your code <-> client-go\n65: \n66: The `v0.x.y` tags indicate that go APIs may change in incompatible ways in\n67: different versions.\n68: \n69: See [INSTALL.md](INSTALL.md) for guidelines on requiring a specific\n70: version of client-go.\n71: \n72: #### Compatibility: client-go <-> Kubernetes clusters\n73: \n74: Since Kubernetes is backwards compatible with clients, older `client-go`\n75: versions will work with many different Kubernetes cluster versions.\n76: \n77: We will backport bugfixes--but not new features--into older versions All are\\n33: // optional.\\n34: type DeltaFIFOOptions struct {\\n35: \\t// If set, log output will go to this logger instead of klog.Background().\\n36: \\t// The name of the fifo gets added automatically.\\n37: \\tLogger *klog.Logger\\n38: \\n39: \\t// Name can be used to override the default \\\"DeltaFIFO\\\" name for the new instance.\\n40: \\tName string\\n41: \\n42: \\t// KeyFunction is used to figure out what key an object should have. (It's\\n43: \\t// exposed in the returned DeltaFIFO's KeyOf() method, with additional\\n44: \\t// handling around deleted objects and queue state).\\n45: \\t// Optional, the default is MetaNamespaceKeyFunc.\\n46: \\tKeyFunction KeyFunc\\n47: \\n48: \\t// KnownObjects is expected to return a list of keys that the consumer of\\n49: \\t// this queue \\\"knows about\\\". It is used to decide which items are missing\\n50: \\t// when Replace() is called; 'Deleted' deltas are produced for the missing items.\\n51: \\t// KnownObjects may be nil if you can tolerate missing deletions on Replace().\\n52: \\tKnownObjects KeyListerGetter\\n53: \\n54: \\t// EmitDeltaTypeReplaced indicates that the queue consumer\\n55: \\t// understands the Replaced DeltaType. Before the `Replaced` event type was\\n56: \\t// added, calls to Replace() were handled the same as Sync(). For\\n57: \\t// backwards-compatibility purposes, this is false by default.\\n58: \\t// When true, `Replaced` events will be sent for items passed to a Replace() call.\\n59: \\t// When false, `Sync` events will be sent instead.\\n60: \\tEmitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\\n61: \\n62: \\t// If set, will be called for objects before enqueueing them. Please\\n63: \\t// see the comment on TransformFunc for details.\\n64: \\tTransformer TransformFunc\\n65: }\\n66: \\n67: // DeltaFIFO is like FIFO, but differs in two ways.  One is that the\\n68: // accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\\n69: // but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\\n70: // object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\\n71: // except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\\n72: // Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\\n73: // not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\\n74: // Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\\n75: // DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\\n76: //\\n77: // The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\\n78: // an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\\n79: // If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\\n80: // replace events for backwards compatibility.  Sync is used for periodic\\n81: // resync events.\\n82: //\\n83: // DeltaFIFO is a producer-consumer queue, where a Reflector is\\n84: // intended to be the producer, and the consumer is whatever calls\\n85: // the Pop() method.\\n86: //\\n87: // DeltaFIFO solves this use case:\\n88: //   - You want to process every object change (delta) at most once.\\n89: //   - When you process an object, you want to see everything\\n90: //     that's happened to it since you last processed it.\\n91: //   - You want to process the deletion of some of the objects.\\n92: //   - You might want to periodically reprocess objects.\\n93: //\\n94: // DeltaFIFO's Pop(), Get(), and GetByKey() methods return\\n95: // interface{} to satisfy the Store/Queue interfaces, but they\\n96: // will always return an object of type Deltas. List() returns\\n97: // the newest object from each accumulator in the FIFO.\\n98: //\\n99: // A DeltaFIFO's knownObjects KeyListerGetter provides the abilities\\n100: // to list Store keys and to get objects by Store key.  The objects in\\n101: // question are called \\\"known objects\\\" and this set of objects\\n102: // modifies the behavior of the Delete, Replace, and Resync methods\\n103: // (each in a different way).\\n104: //\\n105: // A note on threading: If you call Pop() in parallel from multiple\\n106: // threads, you could end up with multiple threads processing slightly\\n107: // different versions of the same object.\\n108: type DeltaFIFO struct {\\n109: \\t// logger is a per-instance logger. This gets chosen when constructing\\n110: \\t// the instance, with klog.Background() as default.\\n111: \\tlogger klog.Logger\\n112: \\n113: \\t// name is the name of the fifo. It is included in the logger.\\n114: \\tname string\\n115: \\n116: \\t// lock/cond protects access to 'items' and 'queue'.\\n117: \\tlock sync.RWMutex\\n118: \\tcond sync.Cond\\n119: \\n120: \\t// `items` maps a key to a Deltas.\\n121: \\t// Each such Deltas has at least one Delta.\\n122: \\titems map[string]Deltas\\n123: \\n124: \\t// `queue` maintains FIFO order of keys for consumption in Pop().\\n125: \\t// There are no duplicates in `queue`.\\n126: \\t// A key is in `queue` if and only if it is in `items`.\\n127: \\tqueue []string\\n128: \\n129: \\t// synced is initially an open channel. It gets closed (once!) by checkSynced_locked\\n130: \\t// as soon as the initial sync is considered complete.\\n131: \\tsynced       chan struct{}\\n132: \\tsyncedClosed bool\\n133: \\n134: \\t// populated is true if the first batch of items inserted by Replace() has been populated\\n135: \\t// or Delete/Add/Update/AddIfNotPresent was called first.\\n136: \\tpopulated bool\\n137: \\t// initialPopulationCount is the number of items inserted by the first call of Replace()\\n138: \\tinitialPopulationCount int\\n139: \\n140: \\t// keyFunc is used to make the key used for queued item\\n141: \\t// insertion and retrieval, and should be deterministic.\\n142: \\tkeyFunc KeyFunc\\n143: \\n144: \\t// knownObjects list keys that are \\\"known\\\" --- affecting Delete(),\\n145: \\t// Replace(), and Resync()\\n146: \\tknownObjects KeyListerGetter\\n147: \\n148: \\t// Used to indicate a queue is closed so a control loop can exit when a queue is empty.\\n149: \\t// Currently, not used to gate any of CRUD operations.\\n150: \\tclosed bool\\n151: \\n152: \\t// emitDeltaTypeReplaced is whether to emit the Replaced or Sync\\n153: \\t// DeltaType when Replace() is called (to preserve backwards compat).\\n154: \\temitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\\n155: \\n156: \\t// Called with every object if non-nil.\\n157: \\ttransformer TransformFunc\\n158: }\\n159: \\n160: // TransformFunc allows for transforming an object before it will be processed.\\n161: //\\n162: // The most common usage pattern is to clean-up some parts of the object to\\n163: // reduce component memory usage if a given component doesn't care about them.\\n164: //\\n165: // New in v1.27: TransformFunc sees the object before any other actor, and it\\n166: // is now safe to mutate the object in place instead of making a copy.\\n167: //\\n168: // It's recommended for the TransformFunc to be idempotent.\\n169: // It MUST be idempotent if objects already present in the cache are passed to\\n170: // the Replace() to avoid re-mutating them. We don't know if the replaced\\n188: \\t// object has changed.\\n189: \\t//\\n190: \\t// NOTE: Previous versions of DeltaFIFO would use Sync for Replace events\\n191: \\t// as well.     "content": "1: /*\n2: Copyright 2014 The Kubernetes Authors.\n3: \n4: Licensed under the Apache License, Version 2.0 (the \"License\");\n5: you may not use this file except in compliance with the License.\n6: You may obtain a copy of the License at\n7: \n8:     http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n9: \n10: Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n11: distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n12: WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n13: See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: package cache\n18: \n19: import (\n20: \t\"errors\"\n21: \t\"fmt\"\n22: \t\"sync\"\n23: \t\"time\"\n24: \n25: \tutilruntime \"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/runtime\"\n26: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/sets\"\n27: \n28: \t\"k8s.io/klog/v2\"\n29: \tutiltrace \"k8s.io/utils/trace\"\n30: )\n31: \n32: // DeltaFIFOOptions is the configuration parameters for DeltaFIFO. All are\n33: // optional.\n34: type DeltaFIFOOptions struct {\n35: \t// If set, log output will go to this logger instead of klog.Background().\n36: \t// The name of the fifo gets added automatically.\n37: \tLogger *klog.Logger\n38: \n39: \t// Name can be used to override the default \"DeltaFIFO\" name for the new instance.\n40: \tName string\n41: \n42: \t// KeyFunction is used to figure out what key an object should have. (It's\n43: \t// exposed in the returned DeltaFIFO's KeyOf() method, with additional\n44: \t// handling around deleted objects and queue state).\n45: \t// Optional, the default is MetaNamespaceKeyFunc.\n46: \tKeyFunction KeyFunc\n47: \n48: \t// KnownObjects is expected to return a list of keys that the consumer of\n49: \t// this queue \"knows about\". It is used to decide which items are missing\n50: \t// when Replace() is called; 'Deleted' deltas are produced for the missing items.\n51: \t// KnownObjects may be nil if you can tolerate missing deletions on Replace().\n52: \tKnownObjects KeyListerGetter\n53: \n54: \t// EmitDeltaTypeReplaced indicates that the queue consumer\n55: \t// understands the Replaced DeltaType. Before the `Replaced` event type was\n56: \t// added, calls to Replace() were handled the same as Sync(). For\n57: \t// backwards-compatibility purposes, this is false by default.\n58: \t// When true, `Replaced` events will be sent for items passed to a Replace() call.\n59: \t// When false, `Sync` events will be sent instead.\n60: \tEmitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n61: \n62: \t// If set, will be called for objects before enqueueing them. Please\n63: \t// see the comment on TransformFunc for details.\n64: \tTransformer TransformFunc\n65: }\n66: \n67: // DeltaFIFO is like FIFO, but differs in two ways.  One is that the\n68: // accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\n69: // but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\n70: // object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\n71: // except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\n72: // Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\n73: // not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\n74: // Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\n75: // DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\n76: //\n77: // The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\n78: // an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\n79: // If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\n80: // replace events for backwards compatibility.  Sync is used for periodic\n81: // resync events.\n82: //\n83: // DeltaFIFO is a producer-consumer queue, where a Reflector is\n84: // intended to be the producer, and the consumer is whatever calls\n85: // the Pop() method.\n86: //\n87: // DeltaFIFO solves this use case:\n88: //   - You want to process every object change (delta) at most once.\n89: //   - When you process an object, you want to see everything\n90: //     that's happened to it since you last processed it.\n91: //   - You want to process the deletion of some of the objects.\n92: //   - You might want to periodically reprocess objects.\n93: //\n94: // DeltaFIFO's Pop(), Get(), and GetByKey() methods return\n95: // interface{} to satisfy the Store/Queue interfaces, but they\n96: // will always return an object of type Deltas. List() returns\n97: // the newest object from each accumulator in the FIFO.\n98: //\n99: // A DeltaFIFO's knownObjects KeyListerGetter provides the abilities\n100: // to list Store keys and to get objects by Store key.  The objects in\n101: // question are called \"known objects\" and this set of objects\n102: // modifies the behavior of the Delete, Replace, and Resync methods\n103: // (each in a different way).\n104: //\n105: // A note on threading: If you call Pop() in parallel from multiple\n106: // threads, you could end up with multiple threads processing slightly\n107: // different versions of the same object.\n108: type DeltaFIFO struct {\n109: \t// logger is a per-instance logger. This gets chosen when constructing\n110: \t// the instance, with klog.Background() as default.\n111: \tlogger klog.Logger\n112: \n113: \t// name is the name of the fifo. It is included in the logger.\n114: \tname string\n115: \n116: \t// lock/cond protects access to 'items' and 'queue'.\n117: \tlock sync.RWMutex\n118: \tcond sync.Cond\n119: \n120: \t// `items` maps a key to a Deltas.\n121: \t// Each such Deltas has at least one Delta.\n122: \titems map[string]Deltas\n123: \n124: \t// `queue` maintains FIFO order of keys for consumption in Pop().\n125: \t// There are no duplicates in `queue`.\n126: \t// A key is in `queue` if and only if it is in `items`.\n127: \tqueue []string\n128: \n129: \t// synced is initially an open channel. It gets closed (once!) by checkSynced_locked\n130: \t// as soon as the initial sync is considered complete.\n131: \tsynced       chan struct{}\n132: \tsyncedClosed bool\n133: \n134: \t// populated is true if the first batch of items inserted by Replace() has been populated\n135: \t// or Delete/Add/Update/AddIfNotPresent was called first.\n136: \tpopulated bool\n137: \t// initialPopulationCount is the number of items inserted by the first call of Replace()\n138: \tinitialPopulationCount int\n139: \n140: \t// keyFunc is used to make the key used for queued item\n141: \t// insertion and retrieval, and should be deterministic.\n142: \tkeyFunc KeyFunc\n143: \n144: \t// knownObjects list keys that are \"known\" --- affecting Delete(),\n145: \t// Replace(), and Resync()\n146: \tknownObjects KeyListerGetter\n147: \n148: \t// Used to indicate a queue is closed so a control loop can exit when a queue is empty.\n149: \t// Currently, not used to gate any of CRUD operations.\n150: \tclosed bool\n151: \n152: \t// emitDeltaTypeReplaced is whether to emit the Replaced or Sync\n153: \t// DeltaType when Replace() is called (to preserve backwards compat).\n154: \temitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n155: \n156: \t// Called with every object if non-nil.\n157: \ttransformer TransformFunc\n158: }\n159: \n160: // TransformFunc allows for transforming an object before it will be processed.\n161: //\n162: // The most common usage pattern is to clean-up some parts of the object to\n163: // reduce component memory usage if a given component doesn't care about them.\n164: //\n165: // New in v1.27: TransformFunc sees the object before any other actor, and it\n166: // is now safe to mutate the object in place instead of making a copy.\n167: //\n168: // It's recommended for the TransformFunc to be idempotent.\n169: // It MUST be idempotent if objects already present in the cache are passed to\n170: // the Replace() to avoid re-mutating them. Default informers do not pass\n171: // existing objects to Replace though.\n172: //\n173: // Note that TransformFunc is called while inserting objects into the\n174: // notification queue and is therefore extremely performance sensitive; please\n175: // do not do anything that will take a long time.\n176: type TransformFunc func(interface{}) (interface{}, error)\n177: \n178: // DeltaType is the type of a change (addition, deletion, etc)\n179: type DeltaType string\n180: \n181: // Change type definition\n182: const (\n183: \tAdded   DeltaType = \"Added\"\n184: \tUpdated DeltaType = \"Updated\"\n185: \tDeleted DeltaType = \"Deleted\"\n186: \t// Replaced is emitted when we encountered watch errors and had to do a\n187: \t// relist, or on initial listing of objects. An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\\n47: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\\n48: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\\n49: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n50: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n51: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n52: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n53: //\\n54: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n55: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n56: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n57: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n58: //\\n59: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n60: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n61: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n62: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n63: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n64: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n65: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n66: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n67: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n68: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n69: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n70: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n71: // selector.\\n72: //\\n73: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n74: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n75: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n76: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n77: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n78: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n79: //\\n80: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n81: // during `Run()`.\\n82: //\\n83: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n84: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n85: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n86: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n87: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n88: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n89: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n90: //\\n91: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n92: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n93: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n94: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n95: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n96: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n97: //\\n98: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n99: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n100: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n101: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n102: // into its constituent parts.\\n103: //\\n104: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n105: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n106: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n107: //\\n108: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\\n109: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\\n110: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\\n111: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\\n112: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\\n113: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\\n114: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\\n115: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\\n116: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\\n117: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\\n118: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\\n119: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\\n120: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n121: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n122: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\\n123: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\\n124: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\\n125: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\\n126: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\\n127: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\\n128: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\\n129: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\\n130: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n131: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n132: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n133: //\\n134: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n135: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\\n136: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\\n137: // to something else, for example through a\\n138: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\\n139: //\\n140: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\\n141: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\\n142: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\\n143: type SharedInformer interface {\\n144: \\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\\n145: \\t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\\n146: \\t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\\n147: \\t// different handlers.\\n148: \\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\\n149: \\t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\\n150: \\t// seen every item in the initial list).\\n151: \\t//\\n152: \\t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\\n153: \\tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\\n154: \\t// AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod adds an event handler to the\\n155: \\t// shared informer with the requested resync period; zero means\\n156: \\t// this handler does not care about resyncs.  The resync operation\\n157: \\t// consists of delivering to the handler an update notification\\n158: \\t// for every object in the informer's local cache; it does not add\\n159: \\t// any interactions with the authoritative storage.  Some\\n160: \\t// informers do no resyncs at all, not even for handlers added\\n161: \\t// with a non-zero resyncPeriod.  For an informer that does\\n162: \\t// resyncs, and for each handler that requests resyncs, that\\n163: \\t// informer develops a nominal resync period that is no shorter\\n164: \\t// than the requested period but may be longer.  The actual time\\n165: \\t// between any two resyncs may be longer than the nominal period\\n166: \\t// because the implementation takes time to do work and there may\\n167: \\t// be competing load To be formally complete, we say that the\n70: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\n71: // selector.\n72: //\n73: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\n74: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\n75: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\n76: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\n77: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\n78: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\n79: //\n80: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\n81: // during `Run()`.\n82: //\n83: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\n84: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\n85: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\n86: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\n87: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\n88: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\n89: // persistently thwart achievement).\n90: //\n91: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\n92: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\n93: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\n94: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\n95: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\n96: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\n97: //\n98: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\n99: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\n100: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\n101: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\n102: // into its constituent parts.\n103: //\n104: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\n105: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\n106: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\n107: //\n108: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\n109: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\n110: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\n111: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\n112: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\n113: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\n114: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\n115: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\n116: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\n117: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\n118: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\n119: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\n120: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\n121: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\n122: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\n123: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n124: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n125: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n126: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n127: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n128: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n129: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\n130: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\n131: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\n132: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\n133: //\n134: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\n135: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\n136: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\n137: // to something else, for example through a\n138: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\n139: //\n140: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\n141: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\n142: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\n143: type SharedInformer interface {\n144: \t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\n145: \t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\n146: \t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\n147: \t// different handlers.\n148: \t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\n149: \t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\n150: \t// seen every item in the initial list).\n151: \t//\n152: \t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\n153: \tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n154: \t// AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod adds an event handler to the\n155: \t// shared informer with the requested resync period; zero means\n156: \t// this handler does not care about resyncs.  The resync operation\n157: \t// consists of delivering to the handler an update notification\n158: \t// for every object in the informer's local cache; it does not add\n159: \t// any interactions with the authoritative storage.  Some\n160: \t// informers do no resyncs at all, not even for handlers added\n161: \t// with a non-zero resyncPeriod.  For an informer that does\n162: \t// resyncs, and for each handler that requests resyncs, that\n163: \t// informer develops a nominal resync period that is no shorter\n164: \t// than the requested period but may be longer.  The actual time\n165: \t// between any two resyncs may be longer than the nominal period\n166: \t// because the implementation takes time to do work and there may\n167: \t// be competing load and scheduling noise.\n168: \t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to remove\n169: \t// the handler again and an error if the handler cannot be added.\n170: \t//\n171: \t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod in code which supports contextual logging.\n172: \tAddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod(handler ResourceEventHandler, resyncPeriod time.Duration) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n173: \t// AddEventHandlerWithOptions is a variant of AddEventHandlerWithResyncPeriod where\n174: \t// all optional parameters are passed in a struct.\n175: \tAddEventHandlerWithOptions(handler ResourceEventHandler, options HandlerOptions) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n176: \t// RemoveEventHandler removes a formerly added event handler given by\n177: \t// its registration handle.\n178: \t// This function is guaranteed to be idempotent, and thread-safe.\n179: \tRemoveEventHandler(handle ResourceEventHandlerRegistration) error\n180: \t// GetStore returns the informer's local cache as a Store.\n181: \tGetStore() Store\n182: \t// GetController is deprecated, it does nothing useful\n183: \tGetController() Controller\n184: \t// Run starts and runs the shared informer, returning after it stops.\n185: \t// The informer will be stopped when stopCh is closed.\n186: \t//\n187: \t// Contextual logging: RunWithContext should be used instead of Run in code which uses contextual logging.\n188: \tRun(stopCh <-chan struct{})\n189: \t// RunWithContext starts and runs the shared informer, returning after it stops.\n190: \t// The informer will be stopped when the context is canceled.\n191: \tRunWithContext(ctx context.Context)\n192: \t// HasSynced returns true if the shared informer's store has been\n193: \t// informed by at least one full LIST of the authoritative state\n194: \t// of the informer's object collection.  This is unrelated to \"resync\".\n195: \t//\n196: \t// Note that this doesn't tell you if an individual handler is synced!!\n197: \t// For that, please call HasSynced on the handle returned by\n198: \t// AddEventHandler.\n199: \tHasSynced() bool\n200: \t// HasSyncedChecker completes if the shared informer's store has been\n201: ",
